NASA\",\"url\":\"https://www.nasa.gov/space-policy-history-documents/\"}]\n\nBased on the search results, here's detailed information about NASA's founding and the National Aeronautics and Space Act of 1958:\n\n## Background and Motivation\n\nThe launch of Sputnik, the world's first artificial satellite, by the Soviet Union in October 1957, and the implied Soviet superiority in missile technology, galvanized the United States. Congress began hearings in November to develop a long-term space plan.\n\n## Legislative Process\n\nIn April 1958, President Dwight D. Eisenhower asked Congress to pass legislation to create a civilian space agency to oversee the nation's space activities. Bipartisan agreement between a Republican President and a Democrat-controlled Congress led to the drafting of legislation to create a civilian agency to oversee America's non-military space activities.\n\nThe House bill passed on June 2 and the Senate version on June 16. Congress passed the final version of the bill, the National Aeronautics and Space Act of 1958, on July 16 and President Eisenhower signed it into law July 29.\n\n## Key Details of the Act\n\nCongress declared that it is the policy of the United States that activities in space should be devoted to peaceful purposes for the benefit of all mankind.\n\nThe \"Space Act,\" as it came to be called, set forth a broad mission for NASA to \"plan, direct, and conduct aeronautical and space activities\"; to involve the nation's scientific community in these activities; and to disseminate widely information about these activities.\n\n## Leadership and Operations\n\nOn Aug. 8, President Eisenhower nominated T. Keith Glennan, president of Case Institute of Technology in Cleveland, Ohio, and Hugh L. Dryden, director of NACA, to be administrator and deputy administrator, respectively. The Senate confirmed them a week later and they were sworn in at the White House on Aug. 19.\n\nThe agency opened for business on Oct. 1, 1958.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"433700f4-fd53-4def-87e7-1be34e6989ca","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:28:17.578Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"2981fec2-d6cd-4c94-b11d-25b4b1c7c2b8","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

However, investigators concluded that the accident had been caused by a turbopump gearbox failure.\n\n## Post-Launch Data\n\nErratic telemetry signals were received from the payload and upper stages for 123 seconds after the explosion, and the upper stages were tracked to impact in the ocean.\n\n## Mission Context\n\nThis spacecraft was the first U.S. attempt at a lunar mission and the first attempted launch beyond Earth orbit by any country. Here are the key launch details:\n\n**Launch Information:**\nPioneer 1 launched on October 11, 1958, as the first spacecraft launched by the newly formed National Aeronautics and Space Administration (NASA).\n\n**Spacecraft Mass:**\nPioneer 1 was launched weighing 84 pounds (38 kilograms).\n\n**Mission Objectives:**\nPioneer 1 was intended to study the ionizing radiation, cosmic rays, magnetic fields, and micrometeorites in the vicinity of the Earth and in lunar orbit.\n\n**Instruments:**\nThe spacecraft carried instruments that measured the radiation surrounding Earth, mapped the total ionizing flux, made the first observations of hydromagnetic oscillations of the magnetic field, and took the first measurements of the density of micrometeorites and the interplanetary magnetic field.\n\n**Orbit/Trajectory:**\nDue to a launch vehicle malfunction, the spacecraft attained only a ballistic trajectory and never reached the Moon. The satellite was 80.75 inches (2.05 meters) long and weighed 30.66 pounds (13.90 kg).\n\n## Instruments\n\nThe main instruments were a cosmic-ray detection package; internal, external and nose-cone temperature sensors; a micrometeorite impact microphone; a ring of micrometeorite erosion gauges; and two transmitters. The primary science instrument on Explorer 1 was a cosmic ray detector designed to measure the radiation environment in Earth orbit.\n\n## Van Allen Belt Discovery\n\nOnce in space, the cosmic ray experiment provided by Dr. James Van Allen of the University of Iowa revealed a much lower cosmic ray count than expected. Van Allen theorized that the instrument may have been saturated by very strong radiation from a belt of charged particles trapped in space by Earth's magnetic field. The existence of these radiation belts was confirmed by another U.S. satellite launched two months later, and they became known as the Van Allen Belts in honor of their discoverer.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"92acf994-6e32-4c53-8304-59d189acf72f","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:28:47.476Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"a296d66d-bd5f-4664-938b-00b837021b10","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Earth Missions - NASA Jet Propulsion Laboratory | NASA Jet Propulsion Laboratory (JPL)\",\"url\":\"https://www.jpl.nasa.gov/missions/explorer-2/\"},{\"title\":\"Explorer 4 - Earth Missions - NASA Jet Propulsion Laboratory | NASA Jet Propulsion Laboratory (JPL)\",\"url\":\"https://www.jpl.nasa.gov/missions/explorer-4/\"}]\n\nBased on the search results from NSSDC (NASA's National Space Science Data Coordinated Archive), here are the details for Explorers 2, 3, and 4 from 1958:\n\n## Explorer 2\nExplorer 2 was launched on March 5, 1958, but this launch failed. With a mass of 14.22 kg, it was about 0.25 kg heavier than Explorer 1. The objective of Explorer 2 was to put a second Explorer satellite in orbit to follow up on the studies of radiation and micrometeorites performed by Explorer 1. The payload included a Geiger-Mueller counter tube for cosmic ray experiments, a high power transmitter, cosmic ray experiment electronics, and a magnetic tape recorder, with an acoustic micrometeorite detector mounted inside the spacecraft cylinder. The fourth stage failed to ignite, and the cause was believed to be due to failure of a light plastic cone that held the igniter in place.\n\n## Explorer 3\nExplorer 3 was launched on March 26, 1958 from Cape Canaveral and was injected into a 186 x 2799 km altitude orbit. With a mass of 14.06 kg, it was about 0.1 kg heavier than Explorer 1. The payload consisted of a cosmic ray counter (a Geiger-Mueller tube), and a micrometeorite detector (erosion gauge). The spacecraft was spin stabilized and had an on-board tape recorder to provide a complete radiation history for each orbit. The satellite decayed from orbit on June 28, 1958, after just over 93 days of operation.\n\n## Explorer 4\nExplorer 4 was launched on July 26, 1958 and was injected into an initial 263 x 2213 km orbit. The on-orbit mass (after fuel burnout) was 17.43 kg. Explorer 4 was instrumented to make the first detailed measurements of charged particles (protons and electrons) trapped in the terrestrial radiation belts and to observe the effects of the Project Argus A-bomb detonations. The spacecraft decayed from orbit after 454 days on October 23, 1959.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"2b572dc9-18a3-478a-8c5f-959b2fc968f4","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:29:11.318Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"cd983b83-bec1-4af9-b96f-e73a9db2e85a","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Shortly after launch, the third stage of the launch vehicle separated but failed to ignite, and Pioneer 2 did not achieve its intended lunar orbit. The spacecraft attained a maximum altitude of 1550 km (963 miles) before reentering Earth's atmosphere at 28.7 N, 1.9 E over NW Africa.\n\n## Pioneer 3\n**Launch Date:** December 6, 1958\n**Launch Vehicle:** Juno II\n**Launch Site:** Cape Canaveral, United States\n**Mass:** 5.87 kg\n\nThe revised spacecraft objectives were to measure radiation in the outer Van Allen belt area using Geiger-Mueller tubes and to test the trigger mechanism for a lunar photographic experiment. The spacecraft failed to go past the Moon and into a heliocentric orbit as planned, but did reach a maximum altitude of over 102,000 km before falling back to Earth.\n\n## Pioneer 4\n**Launch Date:** March 3, 1959\n**Launch Vehicle:** Juno II\n**Launch Site:** Cape Canaveral, United States\n**Mass:** 6.1 kg\n\nIt carried a payload similar to Pioneer 3: a lunar radiation environment experiment using a Geiger-Mueller tube detector and a lunar photography experiment. Although it did not achieve its primary objective to photograph the Moon during a close flyby, Pioneer 4 became the first U.S. spacecraft to reach Earth escape velocity. It also tested a scanning device designed for photographing the earth's cloud cover.\n\n**Paddle Wheel:** Four solar cell paddles mounted near its equator recharged the storage batteries while in orbit. This distinctive design is why it was sometimes referred to as a \"paddlewheel satellite.\"\n\n**Transmitters:** A UHF transmitter was used for the digital telemetry and the TV signal. The launch vehicle was a Thor-Delta and it launched from Cape Canaveral, United States with a mass of 180 kg.\n\n## Design and Physical Characteristics\n\nEcho was NASA's first communications satellite, a passive spacecraft based on a balloon design created by an engineer at the Langley Research Center, made of Mylar, and measured 100 feet (30 meters) in diameter. However, another source notes that it weighed only 132 pounds and consisted of a large sphere with a surface of Mylar plastic covered with vapor-deposited aluminum.\n\n## Function and Purpose\n\nEach spacecraft was a large metallized balloon designed to act as a passive communications reflector to bounce communication signals transmitted from one point on Earth to another. Once in orbit, residual air inside the balloon expanded, and the balloon began its task of reflecting radio transmissions from one ground station back to another.\n\n## Orbit Details\n\nThe \"satelloon\" was folded in a canister, launched in the nose cone of a Thor-Delta rocket, and inflated when it reached orbit 1,000 miles above the earth.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"99006799-1e34-411a-b3bd-4ee7d2951cf9","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:31:30.348Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"15fe6b11-4cf6-4ca5-88e9-27d537231b13","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Spacecraft power was supplied by approximately 9000 1- by 2-cm silicon solar cells mounted on the cover assembly and by 21 nickel-cadmium batteries.\n\n**Orbital Characteristics & Lifespan:**\nTIROS-1 orbited from pole to pole, snapping photos every 30 seconds. The satellite performed normally from launch until June 15, 1960, when an electrical power failure prevented further useful TV transmission.\n\n**Results & Legacy:**\nWithin three months, TIROS-1 generated over 23,000 images of earth and its atmosphere, providing an unprecedented perspective from above and revolutionizing weather forecasting. Part 2 (B)\",\"url\":\"https://www.nasa.gov/history/SP-4001/p2b.htm\"},{\"title\":\"Mercury Redstone 1A - Spacecraft - the NSSDCA\",\"url\":\"https://nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov/nmc/spacecraft/display.action?id=MERCR1A\"}]\n\nBased on the search results, here are the details about the Mercury-Redstone 1 (MR-1) launch failure on November 21, 1960:\n\n## The \"Four-Inch Flight\"\n\nThe first launch attempt of a Mercury-Redstone, designated MR-1, on Nov. 21, 1960, did not go according to plan. After ignition, the Redstone's engine cut off, with the rocket rising about four inches before settling back on the pad.\n\n## Root Cause\n\nThe sequence of events which led to MR-1's difficulties started during the lift-off when the power and control connectors did not disconnect simultaneously. Because of mechanical adjustments the power plug disconnected 29 milliseconds prior to the control plug.\n\nThis permitted part of a 3-amp current, which would have normally returned to ground through the power plug, to pass through the \"normal cutoff\" relay and its ground diode. The cut-off terminated thrust and jettisoned the escape tower.\n\n## Consequences\n\nThe launch escape system's rocket ignited but without taking the capsule with it, followed by deployment of the spacecraft's drogue and main parachutes, with the fully fueled rocket still on the pad.\n\n## Follow-up\n\nBy mid-December 1960, the Redstone team assured Washington that the repeat flight was almost ready: The November 21 type event will be avoided, in the future, by the addition of a ground cable sufficiently long to maintain a good ground connection until all umbilical plugs are pulled. In addition, the booster circuitry has been modified so that a cutoff signal can only get to the capsule after 130 seconds of booster thrust (normal cutoff occurs at 140 seconds).\n\nA second successful attempt, MR-1A, took place on December 19, 1960.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"a166c96f-e51e-44c3-82e5-5024b592fbaa","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:31:54.931Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"feee1de2-806c-4efd-9fd1-845bce850940","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The flight duration was 0 days, 0 hours, 16 minutes, 39 seconds, covering a distance of 422 statute miles at a velocity of 5,857 miles per hour.\n\nDue to the overperformance of the launch vehicle and the added velocity of the escape engine, MR-2 achieved a velocity of about 9,400 km/hour and an altitude of 250 km, with Ham's trip taking 2 minutes and 24 seconds longer than intended and going 670 km downrange (205 km further than planned).\n\n## Ham's Performance and Recovery\n\nHam performed his lever-pulling tasks well in response to flashing lights. Enos became the first chimp to orbit the earth on November 29, 1961, aboard a Mercury Atlas rocket.\n\n## The Chimpanzee\nOn-board was a 17 kg, five-year-old chimpanzee named Enos. Enos performed various psychomotor activities during the flight and was found to be in excellent physical condition following splashdown.\n\n## Mission Duration and Technical Issues\nAlthough the mission plan originally called for three orbits, due to a malfunctioning thruster and other technical difficulties, flight controllers were forced to terminate Enos' flight after two orbits. A metal chip in a fuel supply line caused a problem with the attitude control system, leading to the failure of a roll reaction jet. This resulted in the spacecraft drifting 30 degrees from nominal attitude at which point the automatic controls would bring the spacecraft back to nominal attitude. This entire process caused an extra pound of fuel to be consumed each orbit.\n\n## Recovery and Outcomes\nEnos landed in the recovery area and was picked up 75 minutes after splashdown. He was found to be in good overall condition and both he and the Mercury spacecraft performed well. His mission concluded the testing for a human orbital flight, achieved by John Glenn on February 20, 1962.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"7761a0a9-5ec8-4c6c-b734-5e0de0b66d23","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:32:19.644Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"e6fbad62-2251-44f3-b144-9b8747770cb6","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The launch date was October 3, 1962, from Launch Pad LC-14, with astronaut Walter M. Schirra as the crew member.\n\n## Spacecraft Naming\nSchirra named his capsule Sigma 7, after the Greek letter Σ often used in engineering and mathematics to symbolize summation, and the \"7\" representing the seven Mercury astronauts. Schirra chose Sigma to represent the summation of work by thousands of scientists and engineers across the country to make Project Mercury a reality.\n\n## Orbital Details\nSchirra completed six orbits of the Earth, conducting engineering tests of his spacecraft and several experiments including photography of the planet. Mercury-Atlas 8 was the longest mission NASA had attempted at that time, orbiting the Earth six times in 9 hours and 13 minutes.\n\n## Flight Performance\nDuring the flight, the spacecraft attained a maximum velocity slightly higher than previous flights (28,092 km/hour) and an altitude of about 281 km. The duration of the flight was 9 hours 13 minutes and 11 seconds during which Schirra travelled over 230,000 km.\n\n## Landing\nThe capsule reentered after completing six orbits, landing 440 km northeast of Midway Island in the Pacific Ocean, about 8.2 km from the prime recovery ship, USS Kearsarge.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"bfbcf80a-160f-429f-a12a-a75f7456e3cf","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:33:14.885Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"d15df138-5439-4e19-95f8-6c4d389c6be9","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Gordon Cooper Jr., pilot.\n\n## Mission Duration and Orbits\nHis 22-orbit Mercury-Atlas 9 mission aboard the Faith 7 spacecraft lasted 34 hours and 20 minutes. On May 16, 1963, NASA astronaut L. Gordon Cooper splashed down in the Pacific Ocean, completing the final mission of Project Mercury and the longest American spaceflight up to that time.\n\n## Significance\nIn his single mission, Cooper accumulated more spaceflight time than the other five Mercury flights combined, and gave NASA confidence to proceed to Project Gemini. The Agena B upper stage cut off almost immediately after its ignition for translunar injection instead of firing for 90 seconds, and the cause was traced to a malfunctioning switch which had prematurely choked the flow of red fuming nitric acid to the rocket engine. The probe remained stranded in low Earth orbit and reentered Earth's atmosphere three days later.\n\n## Ranger 2 (November 18, 1961)\n\nRanger 2 experienced a launch failure. Neither Ranger 1 nor 2 left low Earth orbit due to booster problems.\n\n## Ranger 3 (January 26, 1962)\n\nRanger 3 was launched into deep space, but an inaccuracy put it off course and it missed the Moon entirely. A malfunction in the Atlas guidance system (due to faulty transistors) inserted the probe into a lunar transfer trajectory with excessive velocity. A series of malfunctions, principally with the spacecraft's guidance system, sent the spacecraft hurtling past the moon at much higher speeds than planned.\n\n## Ranger 4 (April 23, 1962)\n\nRanger 4 had a perfect launch, but the spacecraft was completely disabled. Ranger 4 suffered technical problems enroute and crashed on the lunar farside, the first U.S. probe to reach the Moon, albeit without returning data.\n\n## Ranger 5 (October 18, 1962)\n\nRanger 5 missed the Moon and was disabled. Ranger 5 lost all solar panel and battery power enroute and eventually missed the Moon.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"6ec108b9-5ba5-41c2-b84c-35e358407737","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:33:38.292Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"827b7b05-d464-4bf7-9032-bfdfa64e8ec7","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The vehicle was destroyed by the Range Safety Officer 293 seconds after launch at 09:26:16 UT when it veered off course.\n\n### Failure Chain of Events\n\nThe booster had performed satisfactorily until an unscheduled yaw-lift (northeast) maneuver was detected by the range safety officer. Faulty application of the guidance commands made steering impossible and were directing the spacecraft towards a crash, possibly in the North Atlantic shipping lanes or in an inhabited area. Six seconds before the Agena and the spacecraft separated from the wayward Atlas, the Range Safety Officer had no choice but to send the destruct command to the Atlas, plunging Mariner 1 into the Atlantic Ocean.\n\n### Root Causes\n\nThe failure resulted from two problems working together:\n\n1. Improper operation of the Atlas airborne beacon equipment resulted in a loss of the rate signal from the vehicle for a prolonged period.\n\n2. A software error, the omission of an overbar for the symbol R for radius (R instead of R̅) in an equation, caused the program to not respond as planned.\n\nThe radio transponder continued to transmit signals for 64 seconds after the destruct command had been sent.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"42022992-79a6-4f4e-859a-20fd82742a59","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:34:19.554Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"490e176a-26c2-45a0-a7f5-075a0e259391","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The specific instruments included a crystal microphone for measurement of the density of cosmic dust and a proton detector for counting low-energy protons in the solar wind.\n\n## First Planetary Flyby Achievement\nMariner 2 became the first successful mission to another planet when it flew by Venus on December 14, 1962.\n\n## Mission Results\nThe spacecraft flew by Venus at a range of 21,660 miles (34,854 kilometers) at 19:59:28 UT Dec. 14, 1962. Scientific discoveries made by Mariner 2 included a slow retrograde rotation rate for Venus, hot surface temperatures and high surface pressures, a predominantly carbon dioxide atmosphere, continuous cloud cover with a top altitude of about 60 km, and no detectable magnetic field.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"91bbf98a-db1c-419d-bf4c-135ca02b95e3","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:34:30.553Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"57bb6ac9-1824-4c06-8298-964cfc05e621","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The mission included the first American spacewalk.\n\n## Gemini V\nGemini V, flown by astronauts L. Gordon Cooper and Charles \"Pete\" Conrad, was the third crewed flight. The mission's primary goals were to evaluate a long-duration space flight of eight days and test rendezvous capabilities. Gemini 8 was launched from Complex 19 at 11:41:02 a.m. EST on 16 March 1966. On March 16, 1966, command pilot Neil Armstrong and pilot David Scott successfully docked their Gemini VIII spacecraft with the Agena target vehicle, the first-ever linking of two spacecraft together. Landing was March 16, 1966, 10:22:28 p.m. EST in the Western Pacific Ocean. On the first day of their mission they completed the first docking in space, but a life-threatening malfunction of the spacecraft's thruster resulted in the mission being aborted soon after.\n\n## Gemini IX\nThe Gemini 9 Augmented Target Docking Adapter (ATDA) was launched from Cape Canaveral using an Atlas-Agena D rocket at 10:00:02 a.m. EST on 1 June 1966. The spacecraft was successfully injected into a near-circular 304 km orbit, but telemetry signals indicated that the launch shroud, which covered the docking adaptor, had separated but had not been jettisoned. This was confirmed when Gemini 9 was launched two days later and the astronauts observed the launch shroud still on the ATDA blocking the docking port.\n\n## Gemini X\nGemini 10 was launched on 18 July from Complex 19 at 5:20:26 p.m. EST and inserted into a 159.9 x 268.9 km orbit. The Gemini X crew of John W. Young and Michael Collins revisited Gemini VIII's Agena target in July 1966 but did not dock with it, making a passive rendezvous with the inactive spacecraft.\n\n## Gemini XII\nGemini XII was the tenth and final crewed flight of the Gemini program, flown by astronauts James A. Lovell Jr. and Edwin E. \"Buzz\" Aldrin Jr. Launch of the Gemini Agena Target Vehicle was at 2:08 p.m. EST and of the Gemini XII mission at 3:47 p.m. EST on Nov. 11, 1966. Total mission elapsed time was 94:34:31. Splashdown occurred in the western Atlantic on Nov. 15, 1966.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"6cd6e3ca-6224-4b2c-9d0f-3a1c19992ba0","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:34:55.783Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"8688eff0-f065-4324-a76b-864a3fd1aa53","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The program consisted of 10 crewed launches and 2 uncrewed launches.\n\n## Key Mission Details\n\n**Early Missions:**\n- Gemini 1, an uncrewed test flight, was launched on 8 April 1964.\n- Gemini 2 flew on a successful suborbital test flight on 19 January 1965.\n- Gemini 3 was launched on March 23, 1965, bearing Virgil I. \"Gus\" Grissom and John W. Young.\n\n**Mid-Program Missions:**\n- Gemini 4 was the second crewed mission of the Gemini series and carried James McDivitt and Edward White on a 4-day, 62-orbit, 98-hr flight from June 3 to June 7, 1965.\n- Launched on August 21, 1965. Astronauts Cooper and Conrad demonstrated long duration spaceflight for 7 days (120 orbits) and setting a world record. (Gemini 5)\n- Launched on 4 December 1965, Commander Frank Borman and Pilot James Lovell Jr. completed a 14 day mission. (Gemini 7)\n- Gemini VI was launched on 15 December 1965. The mission was flown by Walter Schirra, Jr. and Thomas Stafford. (Gemini VI-A)\n\n**Later Missions:**\n- Gemini VIII was launched on 16 March 1966. Neil Armstrong and David Scott performed the first orbital docking with an Agena docking target.\n- Launched on June 3, 1966, Commander Thomas Stafford and Pilot Eugene Cernan, completed a three day, 44 orbit, mission. (Gemini IX)\n- Launched on July 18, 1966. Astronauts Young and Collins performed a rendezvous and docking with the Agena Target Vehicle (Gemini X)\n- Launched on September 12, 1966. Astronauts Conrad and Gordon achieved the first ever first orbit rendezvous and docking with the Agena Target Vehicle. (Gemini XI)\n- Gemini XII: 11-15 November 1966, first fully successful EVA (2 hours 6 minutes).\n\nEach Gemini mission carried two astronauts into Earth orbit for periods ranging from 5 hours to 14 days.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"fa48c255-4c5c-4a49-b8f8-9ea5c20f228c","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:35:27.059Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"20107362-c070-48a8-87a7-f1d240971135","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/media/foxy/ai/GSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

All five missions were successful, and 99% of the Moon was photographed with a resolution of 60 m or better.\n\n## Mission Objectives\n\nThe first three missions were dedicated to imaging 20 potential lunar landing sites, selected based on Earth-based observations. The fourth and fifth missions were devoted to broader scientific objectives and were flown in high altitude polar orbits. Lunar Orbiter 4 photographed the entire nearside and 95% of the farside, and Lunar Orbiter 5 completed the farside coverage and acquired medium (20 m) and high (2 m) resolution images of 36 pre-selected areas.\n\n## Photography Results\n\nThe spacecraft acquired significant numbers of photographs: Lunar Orbiter 1 obtained 42 high-resolution and 187 medium-resolution frames; Lunar Orbiter 2 acquired 609 high-resolution and 208 medium-resolution frames; Lunar Orbiter 3 captured 477 high-resolution and 149 medium-resolution frames; Lunar Orbiter 4 took 419 high-resolution and 127 medium-resolution frames; and Lunar Orbiter 5 returned 633 high-resolution and 211 medium-resolution frames.\n\n## Imaging System\n\nThe Lunar Orbiters had an ingenious imaging system, which consisted of a dual-lens camera, a film processing unit, a readout scanner, and a film handling apparatus. The spacecraft flew by Venus on October 19, 1967.\n\n## Instruments\nScience instruments included a magnetometer, S-band occultation telemetry, UV photometer, trapped radiation detector, dual frequency propagation beacon, Faraday cup, and celestial mechanics. More specifically, the spacecraft conducted seven investigations including: the solar plasma probe to monitor the properties of the solar wind, the helium magnetometer to measure the direction and strength of the magnetic field, the trapped-radiation detector to measure the flux of energetic particles, and the ultraviolet photometer to detect atomic hydrogen and oxygen in Venus' upper atmosphere.\n\n## Key Results\nMariner 5's instruments indicated that the planet's surface temperature and pressure were 981 degrees Fahrenheit (527 degrees Celsius) and 75 to 100 atmospheres, respectively. During the coast to the Moon, one of three thrusters failed to ignite for a course correction, putting the spacecraft into an unwanted spin, and despite 39 repeated attempts to fire the thruster, the engine failed to ignite. Communications ceased just 30 seconds after retro-fire ignition on September 22, 1966, and the spacecraft crashed on the surface of the Moon.\n\n## Surveyor 3\nSurveyor 3 was launched on April 17, 1967 on an Atlas-Centaur from Kennedy Space Center with a launch mass of 1026 kg and a landing mass of 296 kg. The lander settled down to a soft-landing on April 20, 1967, in the southeastern region of Oceanus Procellarum. Surveyor 3 carried a surface-sampling instrument that could reach up to 5 feet from the lander and could dig up to 7 inches deep.\n\n## Surveyor 4\nSurveyor 4 was launched on July 14, 1967 with a landing mass of 283 kg. Radio signals ceased during the terminal-descent phase at 2:02:40 UT on July 17, 1967, approximately 2.5 minutes before planned touchdown.\n\n## Surveyor 5\nSurveyor 5 was launched on September 8, 1967 and landed on September 11, 1967 in Mare Tranquillitatus. Surveyor 5 had magnets attached to the footpads and an alpha scattering instrument for chemical analysis of the lunar material.\n\n## Surveyor 6\nSurveyor 6 was launched on November 7, 1967 and landed on November 10, 1967 in Sinus Medii. Surveyor 6 had magnets attached to the footpads and an alpha scattering instrument.\n\n## Surveyor 7\nSurveyor 7 was launched on January 7, 1968 and landed on January 10, 1968. It was the only spacecraft of the series to land in the lunar highland region and had the most extensive set of instruments.
